codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="85504" data-framerate="25" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/2e/Crisis_salina_del_Messiniense.ogv/Crisis_salina_del_Messiniense.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="228320" data-framerate="25" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/2e/Crisis_salina_del_Messiniense.ogv/Crisis_salina_del_Messiniense.ogv.240p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="143008" data-framerate="25" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="480" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/2e/Crisis_salina_del_Messiniense.ogv/Crisis_salina_del_Messiniense.ogv.360p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="455288" data-framerate="25" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="480" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/2e/Crisis_salina_del_Messiniense.ogv/Crisis_salina_del_Messiniense.ogv.360p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crisis_salina_del_Messiniense.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation: Messinian salinity crisis</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Messinian_salinity_crisis" title="Messinian salinity crisis">Messinian salinity crisis</a></div>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> times, as the northwest corner of Africa converged on Iberia, it lifted the Betic-Rif <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mountain_belt" title="Mountain belt">mountain belts</a> across southern Iberia and northwest Africa. There the development of the intramontane Betic and Rif basins created two roughly parallel marine gateways between the Atlantic Ocean and the Mediterranean Sea. Dubbed the <a href="/wiki/Betic_corridor" title="Betic corridor">Betic</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rifian_corridors&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rifian corridors (page does not exist)">Rifian corridors</a>, they gradually closed during the middle and late Miocene: perhaps several times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[97]</a></sup> In the late Miocene the closure of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Betic_Corridor" title="Betic Corridor">Betic Corridor</a> triggered the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Messinian_salinity_crisis" title="Messinian salinity crisis">Messinian salinity crisis</a>" (MSC), when the Mediterranean almost entirely dried out. The start of the MSC was recently estimated astronomically at 5.96 mya, and it persisted for some 630,000 years until about 5.3 mya;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[98]</a></sup> see Animation: Messinian salinity crisis, at right.
</p><p>After the initial drawdown<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and re-flooding, there followed more episodes—the total number is debated—of sea drawdowns and re-floodings for the duration of the MSC. It ended when the Atlantic Ocean last re-flooded the basin—creating the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a> and causing the <a href="/wiki/Zanclean_flood" title="Zanclean flood">Zanclean flood</a>—at the end of the Miocene (5.33 mya). Some research has suggested that a desiccation-flooding-desiccation cycle may have repeated several times, which could explain several events of large amounts of salt deposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[100]</a></sup> Recent studies, however, show that repeated desiccation and re-flooding is unlikely from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geodynamic" title="Geodynamic">geodynamic</a> point of view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GCV_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-GCV-110">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Desiccation_and_exchanges_of_flora_and_fauna">Desiccation and exchanges of flora and fauna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Desiccation and exchanges of flora and fauna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The present-day Atlantic gateway, the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a>, originated in the early Pliocene via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zanclean_Flood" title="Zanclean Flood">Zanclean Flood</a>. As mentioned, there were two earlier gateways: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Betic_Corridor" title="Betic Corridor">Betic Corridor</a> across southern Spain and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rifian_Corridor&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rifian Corridor (page does not exist)">Rifian Corridor</a> across northern Morocco. The Betic closed about 6 mya, causing the Messinian salinity crisis (MSC); the Rifian or possibly both gateways closed during the earlier <a href="/wiki/Tortonian" title="Tortonian">Tortonian</a> times, causing a "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tortonian_salinity_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tortonian salinity crisis (page does not exist)">Tortonian salinity crisis</a>" (from 11.6 to 7.2 mya), long before the MSC and lasting much longer. Both "crises" resulted in broad connections between the mainlands of Africa and Europe, which allowed migrations of flora and fauna—especially large mammals including primates—between the two continents. The <a href="/wiki/Vallesian" title="Vallesian">Vallesian crisis</a> indicates a typical extinction and replacement of mammal species in Europe during Tortonian times following climatic upheaval and overland migrations of new species:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[103]</a></sup> see Animation: Messinian salinity crisis (and mammal migrations), at right.
</p><p>The almost complete enclosure of the Mediterranean basin has enabled the oceanic gateways to dominate seawater circulation and the environmental evolution of the sea and basin. Circulation patterns are also affected by several other factors—including climate, bathymetry, and water chemistry and temperature—which are interactive and can induce precipitation of <a href="/wiki/Evaporite" title="Evaporite">evaporites</a>. Deposits of evaporites accumulated earlier in the nearby <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_Western_Carpathians#Foredeep" title="Geology of the Western Carpathians">Carpathian foredeep</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Miocene" title="Middle Miocene">Middle Miocene</a>, and the adjacent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Sea_Basin" title="Red Sea Basin">Red Sea Basin</a> (during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Miocene" title="Late Miocene">Late Miocene</a>), and in the whole Mediterranean basin (during the MSC and the <a href="/wiki/Messinian" title="Messinian">Messinian</a> age). Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diatomite" title="Diatomite">diatomites</a> are found underneath the evaporite deposits, suggesting a connection between their<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> formations.
</p><p>Today, evaporation of surface seawater (output) is more than the supply (input) of fresh water by precipitation and coastal drainage systems, causing the salinity of the Mediterranean to be much higher than that of the Atlantic—so much so that the saltier Mediterranean waters sink below the waters incoming from the Atlantic, causing a two-layer flow across the Strait of Gibraltar: that is, an outflow <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar#Inflow_and_outflow" title="Strait of Gibraltar"><i>submarine current</i></a> of warm saline Mediterranean water, counterbalanced by an inflow surface current of less saline cold oceanic water from the Atlantic. In the 1920s, Herman Sörgel proposed the building of a hydroelectric dam (the <a href="/wiki/Atlantropa" title="Atlantropa">Atlantropa</a> project) across the Straits, using the inflow current to provide a large amount of hydroelectric energy. The underlying energy grid was also intended to support a political union between Europe and, at least, the Maghreb part of Africa (compare <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurafrique" title="Eurafrique">Eurafrika</a> for the later impact and <a href="/wiki/Desertec" title="Desertec">Desertec</a> for a later project with some parallels in the planned grid).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Shift_to_a_.22Mediterranean_climate.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Shift_to_a_"Mediterranean_climate"'>Shift to a "Mediterranean climate"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title='Edit section: Shift to a "Mediterranean climate"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The end of the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> also marked a change in the climate of the Mediterranean basin. Fossil evidence from that period reveals that the larger basin had a humid subtropical climate with rainfall in the summer supporting <a href="/wiki/Laurel_forest" title="Laurel forest">laurel forests</a>. The shift to a "Mediterranean climate" occurred largely within the last three million years (the late <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a> epoch) as summer rainfall decreased. The subtropical laurel forests retreated; and even as they persisted on the islands of <a href="/wiki/Macaronesia" title="Macaronesia">Macaronesia</a> off the Atlantic coast of Iberia and North Africa, the present Mediterranean vegetation evolved, dominated by coniferous trees and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sclerophyllous" title="Sclerophyllous">sclerophyllous</a> trees and shrubs with small, hard, waxy leaves that prevent moisture loss in the dry summers. Much of these forests and shrublands have been altered beyond recognition by thousands of years of human habitation. There are now very few relatively intact natural areas in what was once a heavily wooded region.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleoclimate">Paleoclimate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Paleoclimate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Because of its latitude and its landlocked position, the Mediterranean is especially sensitive to astronomically induced climatic variations, which are well documented in its sedimentary record. Since the Mediterranean is subject to the deposition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eolian_dust" title="Eolian dust">eolian dust</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> during dry periods, whereas riverine <a href="/wiki/Detritus_(geology)" title="Detritus (geology)">detrital</a> input prevails during wet ones, the Mediterranean marine <a href="/wiki/Sapropel" title="Sapropel">sapropel</a>-bearing sequences provide high-resolution climatic information. These data have been employed in reconstructing astronomically calibrated time scales for the last 9 Ma of the Earth's history, helping to constrain the time of past <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_reversal" title="Geomagnetic reversal">geomagnetic reversals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[105]</a></sup> Furthermore, the exceptional accuracy of these paleoclimatic records has improved our knowledge of the Earth's orbital variations in the past.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Unlike the vast multidirectional <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">ocean currents</a> in open <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a> within their respective <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_zone" title="Oceanic zone">oceanic zones</a>; <a href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystem" title="Marine ecosystem">biodiversity</a> in the Mediterranean Sea is that of a stable one due to the subtle but strong locked nature of <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">currents</a> which affects favourably, even the smallest macroscopic type of <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">volcanic life form</a>. The stable <a href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystem" title="Marine ecosystem">marine ecosystem</a> of the Mediterranean Sea and <a href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature" title="Sea surface temperature">sea temperature</a> provides a nourishing environment for <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">life in the deep sea</a> to flourish while assuring a balanced <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystem" title="Aquatic ecosystem">aquatic ecosystem</a> excluded from any external <a href="/wiki/Deep_sea" title="Deep sea">deep</a> <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceanic factors</a>. It is estimated that there are more than 17,000 <a href="/wiki/Marine_life" title="Marine life">marine species</a> in the Mediterranean Sea with generally higher marine <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> in <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">coastal areas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelves</a>, and decreases with depth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of the drying of the sea during the <a href="/wiki/Messinian_salinity_crisis" title="Messinian salinity crisis">Messinian salinity crisis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[107]</a></sup> the marine biota of the Mediterranean are derived primarily from the Atlantic Ocean. The North Atlantic is considerably colder and more nutrient-rich than the Mediterranean, and the marine life of the Mediterranean has had to adapt to its differing conditions in the five million years since the basin was reflooded.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Alboran_Sea" title="Alboran Sea">Alboran Sea</a> is a transition zone between the two seas, containing a mix of Mediterranean and Atlantic species. The Alboran Sea has the largest population of <a href="/wiki/Bottlenose_dolphin" title="Bottlenose dolphin">bottlenose dolphins</a> in the Western Mediterranean, is home to the last population of <a href="/wiki/Harbour_porpoise" title="Harbour porpoise">harbour porpoises</a> in the Mediterranean, and is the most important feeding grounds for <a href="/wiki/Loggerhead_sea_turtle" title="Loggerhead sea turtle">loggerhead sea turtles</a> in Europe. The Alboran Sea also hosts important commercial fisheries, including <a href="/wiki/Sardine" title="Sardine">sardines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swordfish" title="Swordfish">swordfish</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_monk_seal" title="Mediterranean monk seal">Mediterranean monk seals</a> live in the Aegean Sea in Greece. In 2003, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a> raised concerns about the widespread <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drift_net" title="Drift net">drift net</a> fishing endangering populations of dolphins, turtles, and other marine animals such as the <a href="/wiki/Galathea_strigosa" title="Galathea strigosa">spiny squat lobster</a>.
</p><p>There was a resident population of <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orcas</a> in the Mediterranean until the 1980s, when they went extinct, probably due to long-term PCB exposure. There are still annual sightings of orca vagrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Specially_Protected_Areas_of_Mediterranean_Importance" title="Specially Protected Areas of Mediterranean Importance">Specially Protected Areas of Mediterranean Importance</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_fish_of_the_Mediterranean_Sea" title="List of fish of the Mediterranean Sea">List of fish of the Mediterranean Sea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_fish_of_the_Black_Sea" title="List of fish of the Black Sea">List of fish of the Black Sea</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_issues">Environmental issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Environmental issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For 4,000 years, human activity has transformed most parts of Mediterranean Europe, and the "humanisation of the landscape" overlapped with the appearance of the present Mediterranean climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yale_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-yale-117">[109]</a></sup> The image of a simplistic, environmental determinist notion of a Mediterranean paradise on Earth in antiquity, which was destroyed by later civilisations, dates back to at least the 18th century and was for centuries fashionable in archaeological and historical circles. Based on a broad variety of methods, e.g. historical documents, analysis of trade relations, <a href="/wiki/Floodplain" title="Floodplain">floodplain</a> sediments, <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>, tree-ring and further archaeometric analyses and population studies, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Thomas_Grove" title="Alfred Thomas Grove">Alfred Thomas Grove</a>'s and <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Rackham" title="Oliver Rackham">Oliver Rackham</a>'s work on "The Nature of Mediterranean Europe" challenges this common wisdom of a Mediterranean Europe as a "Lost Eden", a formerly fertile and forested region, that had been progressively degraded and desertified by human mismanagement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yale_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-yale-117">[109]</a></sup> The belief stems more from the failure of the recent landscape to measure up to the imaginary past of the <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classics</a> as idealised by artists, poets and scientists of the early modern <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yale_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-yale-117">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palomares_H-Bomb_Incident.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="329" data-file-width="425" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Palomares_H-Bomb_Incident.jpg/220px-Palomares_H-Bomb_Incident.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Palomares_H-Bomb_Incident.jpg/330px-Palomares_H-Bomb_Incident.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/db/Palomares_H-Bomb_Incident.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palomares_H-Bomb_Incident.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_bomb" title="Thermonuclear bomb">thermonuclear bomb</a> that fell into the sea recovered off <a href="/wiki/Palomares,_Almer%C3%ADa" title="Palomares, Almería">Palomares, Almería</a>, 1966</div></div></div>
<p>The historical evolution of climate, vegetation and landscape in southern Europe from prehistoric times to the present is much more complex and underwent various changes. For example, some of the deforestation had already taken place before the Roman age. While in the Roman age large enterprises such as the <a href="/wiki/Latifundium" title="Latifundium">latifundia</a> took effective care of forests and agriculture, the largest depopulation effects came with the end of the empire. Some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (September 2014)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> assume that the major deforestation took place in modern times—the later usage patterns were also quite different e.g. in southern and northern Italy. Also, the climate has usually been unstable and there is evidence of various ancient and modern "<a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Ages</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[110]</a></sup> and plant cover accommodated to various extremes and became resilient to various patterns of human activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yale_117-3"><a href="#cite_note-yale-117">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Human activity was therefore not the cause of climate change but followed it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yale_117-4"><a href="#cite_note-yale-117">[109]</a></sup> The wide ecological diversity typical of Mediterranean Europe is predominantly based on human behaviour, as it is and has been closely related to human usage patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yale_117-5"><a href="#cite_note-yale-117">[109]</a></sup> The diversity range<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> was enhanced by the widespread exchange and interaction of the longstanding and highly diverse local agriculture, intense transport and trade relations, and the interaction with settlements, pasture and other land use. The greatest human-induced changes, however, came after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, in line with the "1950s syndrome"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[111]</a></sup> as rural populations throughout the region abandoned traditional subsistence economies. Grove and Rackham suggest that the locals left the traditional agricultural patterns and instead became scenery-setting agents<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> for tourism. This resulted in more uniform, large-scale formations<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup"><span title="Wikipedia:Cleanup">of what?</span></a></i>]</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yale_117-6"><a href="#cite_note-yale-117">[109]</a></sup> Among further current important threats to Mediterranean landscapes are overdevelopment of coastal areas, abandonment of mountains and, as mentioned, the loss of variety via the reduction of traditional agricultural occupations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yale_117-7"><a href="#cite_note-yale-117">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_hazards">Natural hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Natural hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1230" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg/240px-DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg/360px-DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg/480px-DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Stromboli" title="Stromboli">Stromboli</a> volcano in Italy</div></div></div>
<p>The region has a variety of geological hazards which have closely interacted with human activity and land use patterns. Among others, in the eastern Mediterranean, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thera_eruption" title="Thera eruption">Thera eruption</a>, dated to the 17th or 16th century BC, caused a large <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a> that some experts hypothesise devastated the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilisation</a> on the nearby island of Crete, further leading some to believe that this may have been the catastrophe that inspired the <a href="/wiki/Atlantis" title="Atlantis">Atlantis</a> legend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[112]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vesuvius" title="Mount Vesuvius">Mount Vesuvius</a> is the only active <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcano</a> on the European mainland, while others, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Etna" title="Mount Etna">Mount Etna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stromboli" title="Stromboli">Stromboli</a>, are on neighbouring islands. The region around Vesuvius including the <a href="/wiki/Phlegraean_Fields" title="Phlegraean Fields">Phlegraean Fields</a> Caldera west of Naples are quite active<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[113]</a></sup> and constitute the most densely populated volcanic region in the world where an eruptive event may occur within decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vesuvius itself is regarded as quite dangerous due to a tendency towards explosive (<a href="/wiki/Plinian_eruption" title="Plinian eruption">Plinian</a>) eruptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcguire2003_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcguire2003-123">[115]</a></sup> It is best known for its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eruption_of_Mount_Vesuvius_in_AD_79" title="Eruption of Mount Vesuvius in AD 79">eruption in AD 79</a> that led to the burying and destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> cities of <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herculaneum" title="Herculaneum">Herculaneum</a>.
</p><p>The large experience<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of member states and regional authorities has led to exchange<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup"><span title="Wikipedia:Cleanup">of what?</span></a></i>]</sup> on the international level with the cooperation of NGOs, states, regional and municipality authorities and private persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evch_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-evch-124">[116]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Greek%E2%80%93Turkish_earthquake_diplomacy" title="Greek–Turkish earthquake diplomacy">Greek–Turkish earthquake diplomacy</a> is a quite positive example of natural hazards leading to improved relations between traditional rivals in the region after earthquakes in İzmir and Athens in 1999. The European Union Solidarity Fund (EUSF) was set up to respond to major natural disasters and express European solidarity to disaster-stricken regions within all of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[117]</a></sup> The largest amount of funding requests in the EU relates to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forest_fire" title="Forest fire">forest fires</a>, followed by floods and earthquakes. Forest fires, whether man-made or natural, are a frequent and dangerous hazard in the Mediterranean region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evch_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-evch-124">[116]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">Tsunamis</a> are also an often underestimated hazard in the region. For example, the <a href="/wiki/1908_Messina_earthquake" title="1908 Messina earthquake">1908 Messina earthquake</a> and tsunami took more than 123,000 lives in Sicily and Calabria and were among the most deadly natural disasters in modern Europe.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasive_species">Invasive species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Invasive species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Himantura_uarnak_egypt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1592" data-file-width="2123" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Himantura_uarnak_egypt.jpg/220px-Himantura_uarnak_egypt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Himantura_uarnak_egypt.jpg/330px-Himantura_uarnak_egypt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Himantura_uarnak_egypt.jpg/440px-Himantura_uarnak_egypt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Himantura_uarnak_egypt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Reticulate_whipray" title="Reticulate whipray">reticulate whipray</a> is one of the species that colonised the Eastern Mediterranean through the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> as part of the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Lessepsian_migration" title="Lessepsian migration">Lessepsian migration</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The opening of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> in 1869 created the first salt-water passage between the Mediterranean and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>. The Red Sea is higher than the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a>, so the canal functions as a <a href="/wiki/Tidal_strait" title="Tidal strait">tidal strait</a> that pours Red Sea water into the Mediterranean. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitter_Lakes" title="Bitter Lakes">Bitter Lakes</a>, which are hyper-saline natural lakes that form part of the canal, blocked the migration of Red Sea species into the Mediterranean for many decades, but as the salinity of the lakes gradually equalised with that of the Red Sea, the barrier to migration was removed, and plants and animals from the Red Sea have begun to colonise the Eastern Mediterranean. The Red Sea is generally saltier and more nutrient-poor than the Atlantic, so the Red Sea species have advantages over Atlantic species in the salty and nutrient-poor Eastern Mediterranean. Accordingly, Red Sea species invade the Mediterranean biota, and not vice versa; this phenomenon is known as the <a href="/wiki/Lessepsian_migration" title="Lessepsian migration">Lessepsian migration</a> (after <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_de_Lesseps" title="Ferdinand de Lesseps">Ferdinand de Lesseps</a>, the French engineer) or Erythrean ("red") invasion. The construction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aswan_High_Dam" title="Aswan High Dam">Aswan High Dam</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> River in the 1960s reduced the inflow of freshwater and nutrient-rich <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a> from the Nile into the Eastern Mediterranean, making conditions there even more like the Red Sea and worsening the impact of the <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a>.
</p><p>Invasive species have become a major component of the Mediterranean ecosystem and have serious impacts on the Mediterranean ecology, endangering many local and <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> Mediterranean species. A first look at some groups of exotic species shows that more than 70% of the non-indigenous <a href="/wiki/Decapoda" title="Decapoda">decapods</a> and about 63% of the exotic fishes occurring in the Mediterranean are of <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific" title="Indo-Pacific">Indo-Pacific</a> origin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced</a> into the Mediterranean through the Suez Canal. This makes the Canal the first pathway of arrival of <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">alien</a> species into the Mediterranean. The impacts of some Lessepsian species have proven to be considerable, mainly in the Levantine basin of the Mediterranean, where they are replacing native species and becoming a familiar sight.
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> definition, as well as <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Biological_Diversity" title="Convention on Biological Diversity">Convention on Biological Diversity</a> (CBD) and <a href="/wiki/Ramsar_Convention" title="Ramsar Convention">Ramsar Convention</a> terminologies, they are alien species, as they are non-native (non-indigenous) to the Mediterranean Sea, and they are outside their normal area of distribution which is the Indo-Pacific region. When these species succeed in establishing populations in the Mediterranean Sea, compete with and begin to replace native species they are "Alien Invasive Species", as they are an agent of change and a threat to the native biodiversity. In the context of CBD, "introduction" refers to the movement by human agency, indirect or direct, of an alien species outside of its natural range (past or present). The Suez Canal, being an artificial (man-made) canal, is a human agency. Lessepsian migrants are therefore "introduced" species (indirect, and unintentional). Whatever wording is chosen, they represent a threat to the native Mediterranean biodiversity, because they are non-indigenous to this sea. In recent years, the Egyptian government's announcement of its intentions to deepen and widen the canal<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[119]</a></sup> have raised concerns from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_biologist" title="Marine biologist">marine biologists</a>, fearing that such an act will only worsen the invasion of Red Sea species into the Mediterranean, and lead to even more species passing through the canal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrival_of_new_tropical_Atlantic_species">Arrival of new tropical Atlantic species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Arrival of new tropical Atlantic species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In recent decades, the arrival of exotic species from the tropical Atlantic has become noticeable. Whether this reflects an expansion of the natural area of these species that now enter the Mediterranean through the Gibraltar strait, because of a warming trend of the water caused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>; or an extension of the maritime traffic; or is simply the result of a more intense scientific investigation, is still an open question. While not as intense as the "Lessepsian" movement, the process may be of scientific interest and may, therefore, warrant increased levels of monitoring.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea-level_rise">Sea-level rise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Sea-level rise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By 2100 the overall level of the Mediterranean could rise between 3 to 61 cm (1.2 to 24.0 in) as a result of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">effects of climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[121]</a></sup> This could have adverse effects on populations across the Mediterranean:
</p>
<ul><li>Rising sea levels will submerge parts of <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>. Rising sea levels will also mean rising salt water levels in Malta's groundwater supply and reduce the availability of drinking water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[122]</a></sup></li>
<li>A 30 cm (12 in) rise in sea level would flood 200 square kilometres (77 sq mi) of the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a>, displacing over 500,000 <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[123]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> wetlands are also in danger of being destroyed by the rising temperatures and sea levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[124]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Coastal ecosystems also appear to be threatened by <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level rise</a>, especially enclosed seas such as the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic</a>, the Mediterranean and the Black Sea. These seas have only small and primarily east–west movement <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_corridor" title="Habitat corridor">corridors</a>, which may restrict northward displacement of organisms in these areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[125]</a></sup> Sea level rise for the next century (2100) could be between 30 cm (12 in) and 100 cm (39 in) and temperature shifts of a mere 0.05–0.1 °C in the deep sea are sufficient to induce significant changes in species richness and functional diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenpeace.org_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace.org-134">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution">Pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">Pollution</a> in this region has been extremely high in recent years.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (May 2012)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> has estimated that 650,000,000 t (720,000,000 short tons) of <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a>, 129,000 t (142,000 short tons) of <a href="/wiki/Mineral_oil" title="Mineral oil">mineral oil</a>, 60,000 t (66,000 short tons) of mercury, 3,800 t (4,200 short tons) of lead and 36,000 t (40,000 short tons) of <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphates</a> are dumped into the Mediterranean each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[127]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Barcelona_Convention" title="Barcelona Convention">Barcelona Convention</a> aims to 'reduce pollution in the Mediterranean Sea and protect and improve the marine environment in the area, thereby contributing to its sustainable development.'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[128]</a></sup>
Many marine species have been almost wiped out because of the sea's pollution. One of them is the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_monk_seal" title="Mediterranean monk seal">Mediterranean monk seal</a> which is considered to be among the world's most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endangered" title="Endangered">endangered</a> <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">marine mammals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[129]</a></sup> 
The Mediterranean is also plagued by <a href="/wiki/Marine_debris" title="Marine debris">marine debris</a>. A 1994 study of the <a href="/wiki/Seabed" title="Seabed">seabed</a> using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trawl_net" title="Trawl net">trawl nets</a> around the coasts of Spain, France and Italy reported a particularly high mean concentration of debris; an average of 1,935 items per km<sup>2</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nep.org/regionalseas/ma_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-nep.org/regionalseas/ma-138">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plastic_pollution">Plastic pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Plastic pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plastic_debris" title="Plastic debris">Plastic debris</a> accounted for 76% of debris in a 1994 study,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (August 2022)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> of which 94% was <a href="/wiki/Plastic_bag" title="Plastic bag">plastic bags</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (August 2022)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nep.org/regionalseas/ma_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-nep.org/regionalseas/ma-138">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Marine_plastic_pollution" title="Marine plastic pollution">Marine plastic pollution</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Marine_plastic_pollution&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pathway-of-plastic-to-ocean.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2043" data-file-width="3326" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Pathway-of-plastic-to-ocean.png/400px-Pathway-of-plastic-to-ocean.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Pathway-of-plastic-to-ocean.png/600px-Pathway-of-plastic-to-ocean.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Pathway-of-plastic-to-ocean.png/800px-Pathway-of-plastic-to-ocean.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pathway-of-plastic-to-ocean.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The pathway by which plastics enters the world's oceans</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Marine_plastic_pollution" title="Marine plastic pollution">Marine plastic pollution</a> (or plastic pollution in the ocean) is a type of <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">marine pollution</a> by <a href="/wiki/Plastic_pollution" title="Plastic pollution">plastics</a>, ranging in size from large original material such as bottles and bags, down to <a href="/wiki/Microplastics" title="Microplastics">microplastics</a> formed from the <a href="/wiki/Fragmentation_(cell_biology)" title="Fragmentation (cell biology)">fragmentation</a> of plastic material. <a href="/wiki/Marine_debris" title="Marine debris">Marine debris</a> is mainly discarded human rubbish which floats on, or is suspended in the ocean. Eighty percent of marine debris is <a href="/wiki/Marine_debris#Nurdles_and_plastic_bags" title="Marine debris">plastic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marine_plastic_pollution_Weisman_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marine_plastic_pollution_Weisman-139">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[132]</a></sup> Microplastics and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanoplastics" title="Nanoplastics">nanoplastics</a> result from the breakdown or <a href="/wiki/Photodegradation" title="Photodegradation">photodegradation</a> of plastic waste in surface waters, rivers or oceans. Recently, scientists have uncovered nanoplastics in heavy snow, more specifically about 3000 tons that cover Switzerland yearly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[133]</a></sup> 
It is estimated that there is a stock of 86 million tons of plastic marine debris in the worldwide ocean as of the end of 2013, assuming that 1.4% of global plastics produced from 1950 to 2013 has entered the ocean and has accumulated there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[134]</a></sup> It is estimated that 19–23 million tonnes of plastic leaks into aquatic ecosystems annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marine_plastic_pollution_:11_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marine_plastic_pollution_:11-143">[135]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2017_United_Nations_Ocean_Conference" title="2017 United Nations Ocean Conference">2017 United Nations Ocean Conference</a> estimated that the oceans might contain more weight in plastics than fish by the year 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:220px;max-width:220px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beach_plastic_waste_2.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Beach_plastic_waste_2.jpg/218px-Beach_plastic_waste_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Beach_plastic_waste_2.jpg/327px-Beach_plastic_waste_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Beach_plastic_waste_2.jpg/436px-Beach_plastic_waste_2.jpg 2x" width="218"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:220px;max-width:220px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beach_plastic_waste_9.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Beach_plastic_waste_9.jpg/218px-Beach_plastic_waste_9.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Beach_plastic_waste_9.jpg/327px-Beach_plastic_waste_9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Beach_plastic_waste_9.jpg/436px-Beach_plastic_waste_9.jpg 2x" width="218"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">A woman and a boy collecting plastic waste at a beach during a cleanup exercise</div></div></div></div>
<p>Oceans are polluted by plastic particles ranging in size from large original material such as bottles and bags, down to microplastics formed from the fragmentation of plastic material. This material is only very slowly degraded or removed from the ocean so plastic particles are now widespread throughout the surface ocean and are known to be having deleterious effects on marine life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[137]</a></sup> Discarded plastic bags, six pack rings, cigarette butts and other forms of plastic waste which finish up in the ocean present dangers to wildlife and fisheries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[138]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquatic_life" title="Aquatic life">Aquatic life</a> can be threatened through entanglement, suffocation, and ingestion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[141]</a></sup> Fishing nets, usually made of plastic, can be left or lost in the ocean by fishermen. Known as <a href="/wiki/Ghost_net" title="Ghost net">ghost nets</a>, these entangle fish, <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">sea turtles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dugong" title="Dugong">dugongs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabirds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crab" title="Crab">crabs</a>, and other creatures, restricting movement, causing starvation, laceration, infection, and, in those that need to return to the surface to breathe, suffocation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[142]</a></sup> There are various types of ocean plastics causing problems to marine life. Bottle caps have been found in the stomachs of turtles and seabirds, which have died because of the obstruction of their <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">digestive tracts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marine_plastic_pollution_:62_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marine_plastic_pollution_:62-151">[143]</a></sup> Ghost nets are also a problematic type of ocean plastic as they can continuously trap marine life in a process known as "ghost fishing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marine_plastic_pollution_:42_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marine_plastic_pollution_:42-152">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 10 largest emitters of oceanic plastic pollution worldwide are, from the most to the least, China, Indonesia, Philippines, Vietnam, Sri Lanka, Thailand, Egypt, Malaysia, Nigeria, and Bangladesh,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[145]</a></sup> largely through the rivers Yangtze, Indus, Yellow, Hai, Nile, Ganges, Pearl, Amur, Niger, and the Mekong, and accounting for "90 percent of all the plastic that reaches the world's oceans."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[147]</a></sup> Asia was the leading source of mismanaged plastic waste, with China alone accounting for 2.4 million metric tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plastics accumulate because they do not <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegrade</a> in the way many other substances do. They will <a href="/wiki/Photodegradation" title="Photodegradation">photodegrade</a> on exposure to the sun, but they do so properly only under dry conditions, and water inhibits this process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[149]</a></sup> In marine environments, photodegraded plastic disintegrates into ever-smaller pieces while remaining <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a>, even down to the <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecular level</a>. When floating plastic particles photodegrade down to <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> sizes, <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a> attempt to consume them, and in this way the plastic enters the ocean <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
Solutions to marine plastic pollution, along with plastic pollution within the whole environment will be intertwined with changes in manufacturing and packaging practices, and a reduction in the usage, in particular, of single or short-lived plastic products. Many ideas exist for cleaning up plastic in the oceans including trapping plastic particles at river mouths before entering the ocean, and cleaning up the <a href="/wiki/Ocean_gyre" title="Ocean gyre">ocean gyres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marine_plastic_pollution_:10_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marine_plastic_pollution_:10-160">[152]</a></sup></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bottom_trawling">Bottom trawling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Bottom trawling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Bottom_trawling#Environmental_damage" title="Bottom trawling">Bottom trawling § Environmental damage</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bottom_trawling&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<p>Trawling gear produces acute impacts on biota and the physical substratum of the seafloor by disrupting the sediment column structure, overturning boulders, re-suspending sediments and imprinting deep scars on muddy bottoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[153]</a></sup> Also, the repetitive passage of trawling gear over the same areas creates long-lasting, cumulative impacts that modify the cohesiveness and texture of sediments. It can be asserted nowadays that due to its recurrence, mobility and wide geographical extent, industrial trawling has become a major force driving seafloor change and affecting not only its physical integrity on short spatial scales but also imprinting measurable modifications to the geomorphology of entire continental margins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bottom_trawling_researchgate.net_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bottom_trawling_researchgate.net-162">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bottom fishing has operated for over a century on heavily fished grounds such as the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Banks" title="Grand Banks">Grand Banks</a>. While <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a> has long been recognised as causing major ecological changes to the fish community on the Grand Banks, concern has been raised more recently about the damage which benthic trawling inflicts upon seabed communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[155]</a></sup> A species of particular concern is the slow growing, deep water <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">coral</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lophelia_pertusa" title="Lophelia pertusa">Lophelia pertusa</a></i>. This species is home to a diverse community of deep sea organisms, but is easily damaged by fishing gear. On 17 November 2004, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> urged nations to consider temporary bans on high seas bottom trawling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[156]</a></sup> A global analysis of the impacts of bottom trawling found that the impact on seabed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biota_(biology)" title="Biota (biology)">biota</a> was strongly dependent on the type of gear used, with otter trawls estimated as having the smallest impact and removing 6% of biota per pass while hydraulic dredges had the largest impact and removed 41% of biota per pass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[157]</a></sup> Other research found trawled canyon sediments contained 52 percent less organic matter than the undisturbed seafloor. There were 80 percent fewer sea worms in the trawled region and only half as much diversity of species in the trawled seafloor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louisiana_trawling_landsat_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="702" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Louisiana_trawling_landsat_cropped.jpg/220px-Louisiana_trawling_landsat_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Louisiana_trawling_landsat_cropped.jpg/330px-Louisiana_trawling_landsat_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Louisiana_trawling_landsat_cropped.jpg/440px-Louisiana_trawling_landsat_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louisiana_trawling_landsat_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite image of trawler mud trails off the Louisiana coast</div></div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipping">Shipping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Shipping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portacontainer_MSC_in_navigazione_nello_stretto_di_Messina.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2104" data-file-width="3744" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Portacontainer_MSC_in_navigazione_nello_stretto_di_Messina.jpg/220px-Portacontainer_MSC_in_navigazione_nello_stretto_di_Messina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Portacontainer_MSC_in_navigazione_nello_stretto_di_Messina.jpg/330px-Portacontainer_MSC_in_navigazione_nello_stretto_di_Messina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Portacontainer_MSC_in_navigazione_nello_stretto_di_Messina.jpg/440px-Portacontainer_MSC_in_navigazione_nello_stretto_di_Messina.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portacontainer_MSC_in_navigazione_nello_stretto_di_Messina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cargo ship cruises towards the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Messina" title="Strait of Messina">Strait of Messina</a></div></div></div>
<p>Some of the world's busiest shipping routes are in the Mediterranean Sea. In particular, the Maritime Silk Road from Asia and Africa leads through the Suez Canal directly into the Mediterranean Sea to its deep-water ports in <a href="/wiki/Piraeus" title="Piraeus">Piraeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marseilles" title="Marseilles">Marseilles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a>. It is estimated that approximately 220,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merchant_vessel" title="Merchant vessel">merchant vessels</a> of more than 100 <a href="/wiki/Gross_tonnage" title="Gross tonnage">tonnes</a> cross the Mediterranean Sea each year—about one-third of the world's total merchant shipping. These ships often carry hazardous cargo, which if lost would result in severe damage to the marine environment.
</p><p>The discharge of chemical tank washings and oily wastes also represent a significant source of marine pollution. The Mediterranean Sea constitutes 0.7% of the global water surface and yet receives 17% of global marine oil pollution. It is estimated that every year between 100,000 t (98,000 long tons) and 150,000 t (150,000 long tons) of crude oil are deliberately released into the sea from shipping activities.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="743" data-file-width="2540" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg/350px-Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg/525px-Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg/700px-Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Port of <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a></div></div></div>
<p>Approximately 370,000,000 t (360,000,000 long tons) of oil are transported annually in the Mediterranean Sea (more than 20% of the world total), with around 250–300 <a href="/wiki/Oil_tanker" title="Oil tanker">oil tankers</a> crossing the sea every day. An important destination is the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Trieste" title="Port of Trieste">Port of Trieste</a>, the starting point of the <a href="/wiki/Transalpine_Pipeline" title="Transalpine Pipeline">Transalpine Pipeline</a>, which covers 40% of Germany's oil demand (100% of the federal states of Bavaria and Baden-Württemberg), 90% of Austria and 50% of the Czech Republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[159]</a></sup> Accidental <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spills</a> happen frequently with an average of 10 spills per year. A major oil spill could occur at any time in any part of the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenpeace.org_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace.org-134">[126]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kemer_beach,_Antalya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Kemer_beach%2C_Antalya.jpg/220px-Kemer_beach%2C_Antalya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Kemer_beach%2C_Antalya.jpg/330px-Kemer_beach%2C_Antalya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Kemer_beach%2C_Antalya.jpg/440px-Kemer_beach%2C_Antalya.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kemer_beach,_Antalya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kemer" title="Kemer">Kemer</a> Beach in <a href="/wiki/Antalya" title="Antalya">Antalya</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Riviera" title="Turkish Riviera">Turkish Riviera (Turquoise Coast)</a>. In 2019, Turkey <a href="/wiki/World_Tourism_rankings#Most_visited_destinations_by_international_tourist_arrivals" title="World Tourism rankings">ranked sixth in the world</a> in terms of the number of international tourist arrivals, with 51.2 million foreign tourists visiting the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WTO_Tourism_Highlights_2019_Edition_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-WTO_Tourism_Highlights_2019_Edition-169">[161]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The coast of the Mediterranean has been used for tourism since ancient times, as the Roman villa buildings on the <a href="/wiki/Amalfi_Coast" title="Amalfi Coast">Amalfi Coast</a> or in <a href="/wiki/Barcola" title="Barcola">Barcola</a> show. From the end of the 19th century, in particular, the beaches became places of longing for many Europeans and travellers. From then on, and especially after World War II, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_tourism" title="Mass tourism">mass tourism</a> to the Mediterranean began with all its advantages and disadvantages. While initially, the journey was by train and later by bus or car, today the plane is increasingly used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tourism is today one of the most important sources of income for many Mediterranean countries, despite the man-made geopolitical conflicts<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> in the region. The countries have tried to extinguish rising man-made chaotic zones<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> that might affect the region's economies and societies in neighbouring coastal countries, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shipping_route" title="Shipping route">shipping routes</a>. Naval and rescue components in the Mediterranean Sea are considered to be among the best<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> due to the rapid cooperation between various <a href="/wiki/List_of_fleets" title="List of fleets">naval fleets</a>. Unlike the vast open oceans, the sea's closed position facilitates effective naval and rescue missions<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, considered the safest<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and regardless of<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> any man-made or <a href="/wiki/Natural_disaster" title="Natural disaster">natural disaster</a>.
</p><p>Tourism is a source of income for small coastal communities, including islands, independent of urban centres. However, tourism has also played a major role in the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">degradation</a> of the coastal and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_environment" title="Marine environment">marine environment</a>. Rapid development has been encouraged by Mediterranean governments to support the large numbers of tourists visiting the region, but this has caused serious disturbance to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_habitat" title="Marine habitat">marine habitats</a> by erosion and <a href="#Pollution">pollution</a> in many places along the Mediterranean coasts.
</p><p>Tourism often concentrates in areas of high natural wealth<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, causing a serious threat to the habitats of endangered species such as <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">sea turtles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monk_seal" title="Monk seal">monk seals</a>. Reductions in natural wealth may reduce the incentive for tourists to visit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenpeace.org_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace.org-134">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_tourism" title="Environmental impact of tourism">Environmental impact of tourism</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overfishing">Overfishing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Overfishing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">Overfishing</a></div>
<p>Fish stock levels in the Mediterranean Sea are alarmingly low. The European Environment Agency says that more than 65% of all fish stocks in the region are outside safe biological limits and the United Nations Food and Agriculture Organisation, that some of the most important fisheries—such as <a href="/wiki/Albacore" title="Albacore">albacore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_bluefin_tuna" title="Atlantic bluefin tuna">bluefin tuna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hake" title="Hake">hake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marlin" title="Marlin">marlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swordfish" title="Swordfish">swordfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_mullet" title="Red mullet">red mullet</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_bream" title="Sea bream">sea bream</a>—are threatened.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title='Please supply a full or month-and-year "|date=" of publication, or use "|year=year not specified" for intentional omission.'>date missing</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>There are clear indications that catch size and quality have declined, often dramatically, and in many areas, larger and longer-lived species have disappeared entirely from commercial catches.
</p><p>Large open-water fish like tuna have been a shared fisheries resource for thousands of years but the stocks are now dangerously low. In 1999, <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a> published a report revealing that the amount of bluefin tuna in the Mediterranean had decreased by over 80% in the previous 20 years and government scientists warn that without immediate action the stock will collapse.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marine_heatwaves">Marine heatwaves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Marine heatwaves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marine_heatwave" title="Marine heatwave">Marine heatwave</a></div>
<p>A study showed that <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_oceans" title="Effects of climate change on oceans">climate change-related</a> exceptional <a href="/wiki/Marine_heatwave" title="Marine heatwave">marine heatwaves</a> in the Mediterranean Sea during 2015–2019 resulted in widespread mass sealife die-offs in five consecutive years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[164]</a></sup>
</p>

<li><a href="/wiki/Seto_Inland_Sea" title="Seto Inland Sea">Seto Inland Sea</a> – Japanese Inland Sea – also known as the Japanese Mediterranean Sea</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tyrrhenian_Basin" title="Tyrrhenian Basin">Tyrrhenian Basin</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Union_for_the_Mediterranean" title="Union for the Mediterranean">Union for the Mediterranean</a> – Intergovernmental organization</li></ul>
</div>
